class="den">2</span></span><sup>+</sup>)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A <b>proton</b> is a stable <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particle</a>, symbol <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>p<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, H<sup>+</sup>, or <sup>1</sup>H<sup>+</sup> with a positive <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charge</a> of +1 <i>e</i> <a href="/wiki/Elementary_charge" title="Elementary charge">elementary charge</a>. Its mass is slightly less than that of a <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> and 1836 times the mass of an <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton%E2%80%93electron_mass_ratio" title="Proton–electron mass ratio">proton–electron mass ratio</a>). Protons and neutrons, each with masses of approximately one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" title="Atomic mass unit">atomic mass unit</a>, are jointly referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleons</a>" (particles present in atomic nuclei).
</p><p>One or more protons are present in the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> of every <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atom</a>. They provide the attractive electrostatic central force which binds the atomic electrons. The number of protons in the nucleus is the defining property of an element, and is referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> (represented by the symbol <i>Z</i>). Since each <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">element</a> has a unique number of protons, each element has its own unique atomic number, which determines the number of atomic electrons and consequently the chemical characteristics of the element.
</p><p>The word <i>proton</i> is <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> for "first", and this name was given to the hydrogen nucleus by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> in 1920. In previous years, Rutherford had discovered that the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> nucleus (known to be the lightest nucleus) could be extracted from the nuclei of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> by atomic collisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-9">[9]</a></sup> Protons were therefore a candidate to be a fundamental or <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particle</a>, and hence a building block of nitrogen and all other heavier atomic nuclei.
</p><p>Although protons were originally considered elementary particles, in the modern <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> of <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a>, protons are now known to be composite particles, containing three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valence_quark" title="Valence quark">valence quarks</a>, and together with <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> are now classified as <a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">hadrons</a>. Protons are composed of two <a href="/wiki/Up_quark" title="Up quark">up quarks</a> of charge +<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">2</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">3</span></span><i>e</i> and one <a href="/wiki/Down_quark" title="Down quark">down quark</a> of charge −<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">3</span></span><i>e</i>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">rest masses</a> of quarks contribute only about 1% of a proton's mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mass_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mass-10">[10]</a></sup> The remainder of a proton's mass is due to <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chromodynamics_binding_energy" title="Quantum chromodynamics binding energy">quantum chromodynamics binding energy</a>, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> of the quarks and the energy of the <a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluon</a> fields that bind the quarks together.  Because protons are not fundamental particles, they possess a measurable size; the <a href="/wiki/Root_mean_square" title="Root mean square">root mean square</a> <a href="/wiki/Charge_radius" title="Charge radius">charge radius</a> of a proton is about 0.84–0.87 <a href="/wiki/Femtometre" title="Femtometre">fm</a> (or <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6984840000000000000♠"></span>0.84<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−15</sup></span> to <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6984870000000000000♠"></span>0.87<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−15</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meter" title="Meter">m</a></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PSI_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-PSI-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Antognini2013_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Antognini2013-12">[12]</a></sup> In 2019, two different studies, using different techniques, found this radius to be 0.833 fm, with an uncertainty of ±0.010 fm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Free protons occur occasionally on Earth: thunderstorms can produce protons with energies of up to several tens of MeV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kohn2015_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kohn2015-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kohn2017_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kohn2017-16">[16]</a></sup> At sufficiently low temperatures and kinetic energies, free protons will bind to <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>. However, the character of such bound protons does not change, and they remain protons. A fast proton moving through matter will slow by interactions with electrons and nuclei, until it is captured by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_cloud" title="Electron cloud">electron cloud</a> of an atom. The result is a protonated atom, which is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compound</a> of hydrogen. In a vacuum, when free electrons are present, a sufficiently slow proton may pick up a single free electron, becoming a neutral <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">hydrogen atom</a>, which is chemically a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radical" title="Free radical">free radical</a>. Such "free hydrogen atoms" tend to react chemically with many other types of atoms at sufficiently low energies. When free hydrogen atoms react with each other, they form neutral hydrogen molecules (H<sub>2</sub>), which are the most common molecular component of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_clouds" title="Molecular clouds">molecular clouds</a> in <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar space</a>.
</p><p>Free protons are routinely used for accelerators for <a href="/wiki/Proton_therapy" title="Proton therapy">proton therapy</a> or various particle physics experiments, with the most powerful example being the <a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">Large Hadron Collider</a>.
</p>

<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title"><a href="/wiki/Category:Nuclear_physicists" title="Category:Nuclear physicists">Scientists</a></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content"><a href="/wiki/Luis_Walter_Alvarez" title="Luis Walter Alvarez">Alvarez</a> <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/Henri_Becquerel" title="Henri Becquerel">Becquerel</a> <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/Hans_Bethe" title="Hans Bethe">Bethe</a> <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/Aage_Bohr" title="Aage Bohr">A. Bohr</a> <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">N. Bohr</a> <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/James_Chadwick" title="James Chadwick">Chadwick</a> <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/John_Cockcroft" title="John Cockcroft">Cockcroft</a> <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/Ir%C3%A8ne_Joliot-Curie" title="Irène Joliot-Curie">Ir. Curie</a> <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Joliot-Curie" title="Frédéric Joliot-Curie">Fr. Curie</a> <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Curie" title="Pierre Curie">Pi. Curie</a> <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Skłodowska-Curie</a> <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/Clinton_Davisson" title="Clinton Davisson">Davisson</a> <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Fermi" title="Enrico Fermi">Fermi</a> <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/Otto_Hahn" title="Otto Hahn">Hahn</a> <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/J._Hans_D._Jensen" title="J. <ul><li><span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="530" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg/14px-Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg/21px-Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg/28px-Stylised_atom_with_three_Bohr_model_orbits_and_stylised_nucleus.svg.png 2x" width="14"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Physics" title="Portal:Physics">Physics portal</a></span></li>

.navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1105704213">.mw-parser-output .unsolved{margin:0.5em 0 1em 1em;border:#ccc solid;padding:0.35em 0.35em 0.35em 2.2em;background-color:#eee;background-image:url("https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/26/Question%2C_Web_Fundamentals.svg");background-position:top 50%left 0.35em;background-size:1.5em;background-repeat:no-repeat}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .unsolved{clear:right;float:right;max-width:25%}}.mw-parser-output .unsolved-label{font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .unsolved-body{margin:0.35em;font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output .unsolved-more{font-size:smaller}</style>
<div aria-labelledby="unsolved-label-physics" class="unsolved" role="note">
<div><span class="unsolved-label" id="unsolved-label-physics">Unsolved problem in physics</span>:</div>
<p class="unsolved-body">How do the quarks and gluons carry the spin of protons?</p>
<div class="unsolved-more"><a href="/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="List of unsolved problems in physics">(more unsolved problems in physics)</a></div>
</div>
<p>Protons are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin-%C2%BD" title="Spin-½">spin-<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span></a> <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermions</a> and are composed of three valence quarks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adair1989_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adair1989-17">[17]</a></sup> making them <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryons</a> (a sub-type of <a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">hadrons</a>). The two <a href="/wiki/Up_quark" title="Up quark">up quarks</a> and one <a href="/wiki/Down_quark" title="Down quark">down quark</a> of a proton are held together by the <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">strong force</a>, mediated by <a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cottingham1986_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cottingham1986-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 21–22">: 21–22 </span></sup> A modern perspective has a proton composed of the valence quarks (up, up, down), the gluons, and transitory pairs of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_quark" title="Sea quark">sea quarks</a>. Protons have a positive charge distribution which decays approximately exponentially, with a root mean square <a href="/wiki/Charge_radius" title="Charge radius">charge radius</a> of about 0.8 fm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Basdevant2005_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Basdevant2005-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Protons and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> are both <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleons</a>, which may be bound together by the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">nuclear force</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nuclei</a>. The nucleus of the most common <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">hydrogen atom</a> (with the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_symbol" title="Chemical symbol">chemical symbol</a> "H") is a lone proton. The nuclei of the heavy hydrogen isotopes <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> contain one proton bound to one and two neutrons, respectively. All other types of atomic nuclei are composed of two or more protons and various numbers of neutrons.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The concept of a hydrogen-like particle as a constituent of other atoms was developed over a long period. As early as 1815, <a href="/wiki/William_Prout" title="William Prout">William Prout</a> proposed that all atoms are composed of hydrogen atoms (which he called "protyles"), based on a simplistic interpretation of early values of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight">atomic weights</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Prout%27s_hypothesis" title="Prout's hypothesis">Prout's hypothesis</a>), which was disproved when more accurate values were measured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lecturer2006_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lecturer2006-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 39–42">: 39–42 </span></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rutherford_1911_Solvay.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="297" data-file-width="212" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Rutherford_1911_Solvay.jpg/150px-Rutherford_1911_Solvay.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3b/Rutherford_1911_Solvay.jpg 1.5x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rutherford_1911_Solvay.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> at the first <a href="/wiki/Solvay_Conference" title="Solvay Conference">Solvay Conference</a>, 1911</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:82px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Proton_detected_in_an_isopropanol_cloud_chamber.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="80" data-file-width="80" decoding="async" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/Proton_detected_in_an_isopropanol_cloud_chamber.jpg" width="80"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Proton_detected_in_an_isopropanol_cloud_chamber.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Proton detected in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isopropanol" title="Isopropanol">isopropanol</a> <a href="/wiki/Cloud_chamber" title="Cloud chamber">cloud chamber</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1886, <a href="/wiki/Eugen_Goldstein" title="Eugen Goldstein">Eugen Goldstein</a> discovered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canal_rays" title="Canal rays">canal rays</a> (also known as anode rays) and showed that they were positively charged particles (ions) produced from gases. However, since particles from different gases had different values of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charge-to-mass_ratio" title="Charge-to-mass ratio">charge-to-mass ratio</a> (e/m), they could not be identified with a single particle, unlike the negative <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> discovered by <a href="/wiki/J._J._Thomson" title="J. J. Thomson">J. J. Thomson</a>. <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Wien" title="Wilhelm Wien">Wilhelm Wien</a> in 1898 identified the hydrogen ion as the particle with the highest charge-to-mass ratio in ionized gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wien1904_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wien1904-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the discovery of the atomic nucleus by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> in 1911, <a href="/wiki/Antonius_van_den_Broek" title="Antonius van den Broek">Antonius van den Broek</a> proposed that the place of each element in the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a> (its atomic number) is equal to its nuclear charge. This was confirmed experimentally by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Moseley" title="Henry Moseley">Henry Moseley</a> in 1913 using <a href="/wiki/X-ray_spectroscopy" title="X-ray spectroscopy">X-ray spectra</a>.
</p><p>In 1917 (in experiments reported in 1919 and 1925), Rutherford proved that the hydrogen nucleus is present in other nuclei, a result usually described as the discovery of protons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Petrucci2002_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Petrucci2002-22">[22]</a></sup> These experiments began after Rutherford had noticed that, when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha_particles" title="Alpha particles">alpha particles</a> were shot into air (mostly nitrogen), his scintillation detectors showed the signatures of typical hydrogen nuclei as a product. After experimentation Rutherford traced the reaction to the nitrogen in air and found that when alpha particles were introduced into pure nitrogen gas, the effect was larger. In 1919 Rutherford assumed that the alpha particle merely knocked a proton out of nitrogen, turning it into carbon. After observing Blackett's cloud chamber images in 1925, Rutherford realized that the alpha particle was absorbed. After capture of the alpha particle, a hydrogen nucleus is ejected, so that heavy oxygen, not carbon, is the result – i.e., the atomic number <i>Z</i> of the nucleus is increased rather than reduced (see initial proposed reaction below). This was the first reported <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reaction</a>, <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1060974363">.mw-parser-output .template-chem2-su{display:inline-block;font-size:80%;line-height:1;vertical-align:-0.35em}.mw-parser-output .template-chem2-su>span{display:block}.mw-parser-output sub.template-chem2-sub{font-size:80%;vertical-align:-0.35em}.mw-parser-output sup.template-chem2-sup{font-size:80%;vertical-align:0.65em}</style><span class="chemf nowrap"><sup class="template-chem2-sup">14</sup>N + α → <sup class="template-chem2-sup">17</sup>O + p</span>. Rutherford at first thought of our modern "p" in this equation as a hydrogen ion, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sup class="template-chem2-sup">+</sup></span>.
</p><p>Depending on one's perspective, either 1919 (when it was seen experimentally as derived from another source than hydrogen) or 1920 (when it was recognized and proposed as an elementary particle) may be regarded as the moment when the proton was 'discovered'.
</p><p>Rutherford knew hydrogen to be the simplest and lightest element and was influenced by <a href="/wiki/Prout%27s_hypothesis" title="Prout's hypothesis">Prout's hypothesis</a> that hydrogen was the building block of all elements. Discovery that the hydrogen nucleus is present in other nuclei as an elementary particle led Rutherford to give the hydrogen nucleus <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sup class="template-chem2-sup">+</sup></span> a special name as a particle, since he suspected that hydrogen, the lightest element, contained only one of these particles. He named this new fundamental building block of the nucleus the <i>proton,</i> after the neuter singular of the Greek word for "first", <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">πρῶτον</span></span>. However, Rutherford also had in mind the word <i>protyle</i> as used by Prout. Rutherford spoke at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Science" title="British Association for the Advancement of Science">British Association for the Advancement of Science</a> at its <a href="/wiki/Cardiff" title="Cardiff">Cardiff</a> meeting beginning 24 August 1920.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatureScience_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatureScience-23">[23]</a></sup> Rutherford first proposed (wrongly, see above) that this nitrogen reaction was <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap"><sup class="template-chem2-sup">14</sup>N + α → <sup class="template-chem2-sup">14</sup>C + α + H<sup class="template-chem2-sup">+</sup></span>. At the meeting, he was asked by <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Lodge" title="Oliver Lodge">Oliver Lodge</a> for a new name for the positive hydrogen nucleus to avoid confusion with the neutral hydrogen atom. He initially suggested both <i>proton</i> and <i>prouton</i> (after Prout).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romer1997_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Romer1997-24">[24]</a></sup> Rutherford later reported that the meeting had accepted his suggestion that the hydrogen nucleus be named the "proton", following Prout's word "protyle".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Footnote_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Footnote-25">[25]</a></sup> The first use of the word "proton" in the scientific literature appeared in 1920.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pais1986_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pais1986-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Stability">Stability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Stability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proton_decay" title="Proton decay">Proton decay</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1105704213" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
<div aria-labelledby="unsolved-label-physics" class="unsolved" role="note">
<div><span class="unsolved-label" id="unsolved-label-physics">Unsolved problem in physics</span>:</div>
<p class="unsolved-body">Are protons fundamentally stable? Or do they decay with a finite lifetime as predicted by some extensions to the standard model?</p>
<div class="unsolved-more"><a href="/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="List of unsolved problems in physics">(more unsolved problems in physics)</a></div>
</div>
<p>The free proton (a proton not bound to nucleons or electrons) is a stable particle that has not been observed to break down spontaneously to other particles. Free protons are found naturally in a number of situations in which energies or temperatures are high enough to separate them from electrons, for which they have some affinity. Free protons exist in <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasmas</a> in which temperatures are too high to allow them to combine with <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>. Free protons of high energy and velocity make up 90% of <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>, which propagate in vacuum for interstellar distances. Free protons are <a href="/wiki/Proton_emission" title="Proton emission">emitted directly</a> from <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nuclei</a> in some rare types of <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a>. Protons also result (along with electrons and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antineutrino" title="Antineutrino">antineutrinos</a>) from the <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> of free neutrons, which are unstable.
</p><p>The spontaneous decay of free protons has never been observed, and protons are therefore considered stable particles according to the Standard Model. However, some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_unified_theory" title="Grand unified theory">grand unified theories</a> (GUTs) of particle physics predict that <a href="/wiki/Proton_decay" title="Proton decay">proton decay</a> should take place with lifetimes between 10<sup>31</sup> to 10<sup>36</sup> years and experimental searches have established lower bounds on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mean_lifetime" title="Mean lifetime">mean lifetime</a> of a proton for various assumed decay products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bucella1989_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bucella1989-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee1995_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lee1995-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kamioka_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kamioka-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Experiments at the <a href="/wiki/Super-Kamiokande" title="Super-Kamiokande">Super-Kamiokande</a> detector in Japan gave lower limits for proton <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mean_lifetime" title="Mean lifetime">mean lifetime</a> of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7041208280159999999♠"></span>6.6<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>33</sup> years</span> for decay to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antimuon" title="Antimuon">antimuon</a> and a neutral <a href="/wiki/Pion" title="Pion">pion</a>, and <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7041258772319999999♠"></span>8.2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>33</sup> years</span> for decay to a <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a> and a neutral pion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nishino2009_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nishino2009-31">[31]</a></sup>
Another experiment at the <a href="/wiki/Sudbury_Neutrino_Observatory" title="Sudbury Neutrino Observatory">Sudbury Neutrino Observatory</a> in Canada searched for <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a> resulting from residual nuclei resulting from the decay of a proton from oxygen-16. This experiment was designed to detect decay to any product, and established a lower limit to a proton lifetime of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7036662709600000000♠"></span>2.1<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>29</sup> years</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ahmed2004_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed2004-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, protons are known to transform into <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> through the process of <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> (also called <a href="/wiki/Inverse_beta_decay" title="Inverse beta decay">inverse beta decay</a>). For free protons, this process does not occur spontaneously but only when energy is supplied. The equation is:
</p>
<dl><dd><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>p<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span> → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Electron_neutrino" title="Electron neutrino"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>ν<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></a></span></dd></dl>
<p>The process is reversible; neutrons can convert back to protons through <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>, a common form of <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a>. In fact, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_neutron" title="Free neutron">free neutron</a> decays this way, with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mean_lifetime" title="Mean lifetime">mean lifetime</a> of about 15 minutes. A proton can also transform into neutrons through <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission" title="Positron emission">beta plus decay</a> (β+ decay).
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a>, the mean proper lifetime of protons <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \tau _{\mathrm {p} }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

Acceleration gives rise to a <a href="/wiki/S-matrix" title="S-matrix">non-vanishing probability</a> for the transition <span class="nowrap"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span></span>. This was a matter of concern in the later 1990s because <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \tau _{\mathrm {p} }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \tau _{\mathrm {p} }}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \tau _{\mathrm {p} }}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/aba67c1e81ab0679738eb51bfb7c62c7832951b0" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:2.162ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> is a scalar that can be measured by the inertial and <a href="/wiki/Rindler_coordinates" title="Rindler coordinates">coaccelerated observers</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Inertial_frame_of_reference" title="Inertial frame of reference">inertial frame</a>, the accelerating proton should decay according to the formula above. However, according to the coaccelerated observer the proton is at rest and hence should not decay. This puzzle is solved by realizing that in the coaccelerated frame there is a thermal bath due to <a href="/wiki/Unruh_effect" title="Unruh effect">Fulling–Davies–Unruh effect</a>, an intrinsic effect of quantum field theory. In this thermal bath, experienced by the proton, there are electrons and antineutrinos with which the proton may interact according to the processes: (i) <span class="nowrap"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></span>, (ii) <span class="nowrap"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">ν</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></span> and (iii) <span class="nowrap"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup Adding the contributions of each of these processes, one should obtain <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \tau _{\mathrm {p} }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Quarks_and_the_mass_of_a_proton">Quarks and the mass of a proton</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Quarks and the mass of a proton">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chromodynamics" title="Quantum chromodynamics">quantum chromodynamics</a>, the modern theory of the nuclear force, most of the mass of protons and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> is explained by <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a>. The mass of a proton is about 80–100 times greater than the sum of the rest masses of its three valence <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluons</a> have zero rest mass. The extra energy of the <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluons</a> in a proton, as compared to the rest energy of the quarks alone in the <a href="/wiki/QCD_vacuum" title="QCD vacuum">QCD vacuum</a>, accounts for almost 99% of the proton's mass. The rest mass of a proton is, thus, the <a href="/wiki/Invariant_mass" title="Invariant mass">invariant mass</a> of the system of moving quarks and gluons that make up the particle, and, in such systems, even the energy of massless particles is <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence#Fast-moving_objects_and_systems_of_objects" title="Mass–energy equivalence">still measured</a> as part of the rest mass of the system.
</p><p>Two terms are used in referring to the mass of the quarks that make up protons: <i><a href="/wiki/Current_quark" title="Current quark">current quark</a> mass</i> refers to the mass of a quark by itself, while <i><a href="/wiki/Constituent_quark" title="Constituent quark">constituent quark</a> mass</i> refers to the current quark mass plus the mass of the <a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluon</a> <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">particle field</a> surrounding the quark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waston2004_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waston2004-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 285–286">: 285–286 </span></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2003_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2003-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 150–151">: 150–151 </span></sup> These masses typically have very different values. The kinetic energy of the quarks that is a consequence of confinement is a contribution (see <i><a href="/wiki/Mass_in_special_relativity" title="Mass in special relativity">Mass in special relativity</a></i>).  Using <a href="/wiki/Lattice_QCD" title="Lattice QCD">lattice QCD</a> calculations, the contributions to the mass of the proton are the quark condensate (~9%, comprising the up and down quarks and a sea of virtual strange quarks), the quark kinetic energy (~32%), the gluon kinetic energy (~37%), and the anomalous gluonic contribution (~23%, comprising contributions from condensates of all quark flavors).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The constituent quark model wavefunction for the proton is
</p>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathrm {|p_{\uparrow }\rangle ={\frac {1}{\sqrt {18}}}[2|u_{\uparrow }d_{\downarrow }u_{\uparrow }\rangle +2|u_{\uparrow }u_{\uparrow }d_{\downarrow }\rangle +2|d_{\downarrow }u_{\uparrow }u_{\uparrow }\rangle -|u_{\uparrow }u_{\downarrow }d_{\uparrow }\rangle -|u_{\uparrow }d_{\uparrow }u_{\downarrow }\rangle -|u_{\downarrow }d_{\uparrow }u_{\uparrow }\rangle -|d_{\uparrow }u_{\downarrow }u_{\uparrow }\rangle -|d_{\uparrow }u_{\uparrow }u_{\downarrow }\rangle -|u_{\downarrow }u_{\uparrow }d_{\uparrow }\rangle ]} .}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ae0f4cf0fb1b1a50f729084cb9c421245ad88da7" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:117.571ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>The internal dynamics of protons are complicated, because they are determined by the quarks' exchanging gluons, and interacting with various vacuum condensates. <a href="/wiki/Lattice_QCD" title="Lattice QCD">Lattice QCD</a> provides a way of calculating the mass of a proton directly from the theory to any accuracy, in principle. The most recent calculations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SMAnswers_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-SMAnswers-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fodor2008_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fodor2008-41">[41]</a></sup> claim that the mass is determined to better than 4% accuracy, even to 1% accuracy (see Figure S5 in Dürr <i>et al.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fodor2008_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fodor2008-41">[41]</a></sup>). These claims are still controversial, because the calculations cannot yet be done with quarks as light as they are in the real world. This means that the predictions are found by a process of <a href="/wiki/Extrapolation" title="Extrapolation">extrapolation</a>, which can introduce systematic errors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perdrisat2007_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perdrisat2007-42">[42]</a></sup> It is hard to tell whether these errors are controlled properly, because the quantities that are compared to experiment are the masses of the <a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">hadrons</a>, which are known in advance.
</p><p>These recent calculations are performed by massive supercomputers, and, as noted by Boffi and Pasquini: "a detailed description of the nucleon structure is still missing because ... long-distance behavior requires a nonperturbative and/or numerical treatment ..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boffi2007_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boffi2007-43">[43]</a></sup>
More conceptual approaches to the structure of protons are: the <a href="/wiki/Skyrmion" title="Skyrmion">topological soliton</a> approach originally due to <a href="/wiki/Tony_Skyrme" title="Tony Skyrme">Tony Skyrme</a> and the more accurate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AdS/QCD" title="AdS/QCD">AdS/QCD approach</a> that extends it to include a <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a> of gluons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erlich2008_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Erlich2008-44">[44]</a></sup> various QCD-inspired models like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bag_model" title="Bag model">bag model</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Constituent_quark" title="Constituent quark">constituent quark</a> model, which were popular in the 1980s, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SVZ_sum_rules" title="SVZ sum rules">SVZ sum rules</a>, which allow for rough approximate mass calculations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calangelo2000_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calangelo2000-45">[45]</a></sup> These methods do not have the same accuracy as the more brute-force lattice QCD methods, at least not yet.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Charge_radius">Charge radius</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Charge radius">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proton_radius_puzzle" title="Proton radius puzzle">Proton radius puzzle</a></div>
<p>The problem of defining a radius for an atomic nucleus (proton) is similar to the problem of <a href="/wiki/Atomic_radius" title="Atomic radius">atomic radius</a>, in that neither atoms nor their nuclei have definite boundaries. However, the nucleus can be modeled as a sphere of positive charge for the interpretation of <a href="/wiki/Electron_scattering" title="Electron scattering">electron scattering</a> experiments: because there is no definite boundary to the nucleus, the electrons "see" a range of cross-sections, for which a mean can be taken. The qualification of "rms" (for "<a href="/wiki/Root_mean_square" title="Root mean square">root mean square</a>") arises because it is the nuclear cross-section, proportional to the square of the radius, which is determining for electron scattering.
</p><p>The internationally accepted value of a proton's <a href="/wiki/Charge_radius" title="Charge radius">charge radius</a> is <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6984876800000000000♠"></span>0.8768 <a href="/wiki/Femtometre" title="Femtometre">fm</a></span> (see <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(length)#1E-15" title="Orders of magnitude (length)">orders of magnitude</a> for comparison to other sizes). This value is based on measurements involving a proton and an electron (namely, <a href="/wiki/Electron_scattering" title="Electron scattering">electron scattering</a> measurements and complex calculation involving scattering cross section based on <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Rosenbluth" title="Marshall Rosenbluth">Rosenbluth</a> equation for <a href="/wiki/Momentum-transfer_cross_section" title="Momentum-transfer cross section">momentum-transfer cross section</a>), and studies of the atomic <a href="/wiki/Energy_level" title="Energy level">energy levels</a> of hydrogen and deuterium.
</p><p>However, in 2010 an international research team published a proton charge radius measurement via the <a href="/wiki/Lamb_shift" title="Lamb shift">Lamb shift</a> in muonic hydrogen (an <a href="/wiki/Exotic_atom" title="Exotic atom">exotic atom</a> made of a proton and a negatively charged <a href="/wiki/Muon" title="Muon">muon</a>). As a muon is 200 times heavier than an electron, its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Broglie_wavelength" title="De Broglie wavelength">de Broglie wavelength</a> is correspondingly shorter.  This smaller <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">atomic orbital</a> is much more sensitive to the proton's charge radius, so allows more precise measurement.  Their measurement of the <a href="/wiki/Root_mean_square" title="Root mean square">root-mean-square</a> charge radius of a proton is "<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6984841840000000000♠"></span>0.841<span style="margin-left:.25em;">84</span>(67) fm</span>, which differs by 5.0 <a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">standard deviations</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CODATA" title="CODATA">CODATA</a> value of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6984876800000000000♠"></span>0.8768(69) fm</span>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pohl2010_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pohl2010-46">[46]</a></sup> In January 2013, an updated value for the charge radius of a proton—<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6984840870000000000♠"></span>0.840<span style="margin-left:.25em;">87</span>(39) fm</span>—was published. The precision was improved by 1.7 times, increasing the significance of the discrepancy to 7<i>σ</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Antognini2013_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Antognini2013-12">[12]</a></sup>
The 2014 CODATA adjustment slightly reduced the recommended value for the proton radius (computed using electron measurements only) to <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6984875100000000000♠"></span>0.8751(61) fm</span>, but this leaves the discrepancy at 5.6<i>σ</i>.
</p><p>If no errors were found in the measurements or calculations, it would have been necessary to re-examine the world's most precise and best-tested fundamental theory: <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">quantum electrodynamics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AZONANO_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-AZONANO-47">[47]</a></sup> The proton radius was a puzzle as of 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conover2017_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Conover2017-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlson2015_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson2015-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>A resolution came in 2019, when two different studies, using different techniques involving the <a href="/wiki/Lamb_shift" title="Lamb shift">Lamb shift</a> of the electron in hydrogen, and electron–proton scattering, found the radius of the proton to be 0.833 fm, with an uncertainty of ±0.010 fm, and 0.831 fm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The radius of the proton is linked to the form factor and <a href="/wiki/Momentum-transfer_cross_section" title="Momentum-transfer cross section">momentum-transfer cross section</a>. The atomic form factor <i>G</i> modifies the cross section corresponding to point-like proton.
</p>

<p>The form factor can be split in electric and magnetic form factors. These can be further written as linear combinations of Dirac and Pauli form factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlson2015_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson2015-49">[49]</a></sup>
