<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pressure_inside_the_proton">Pressure inside the proton</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Pressure inside the proton">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the proton is composed of quarks confined by gluons, an equivalent <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> which acts on the quarks can be defined. This allows calculation of their distribution as a function of distance from the centre using <a href="/wiki/Compton_scattering" title="Compton scattering">Compton scattering</a> of high-energy electrons (DVCS, for <i>deeply virtual Compton scattering</i>). The pressure is maximum at the centre, about 10<sup>35</sup> Pa, which is greater than the pressure inside a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron star</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burkert2018_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert2018-50">[50]</a></sup> It is positive (repulsive) to a radial distance of about 0.6 fm, negative (attractive) at greater distances, and very weak beyond about 2 fm.
</p>
<h3><span id="Charge_radius_in_solvated_proton.2C_hydronium"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Charge_radius_in_solvated_proton,_hydronium">Charge radius in solvated proton, hydronium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Charge radius in solvated proton, hydronium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The radius of the hydrated proton appears in the <a href="/wiki/Born_equation" title="Born equation">Born equation</a> for calculating the hydration enthalpy of <a href="/wiki/Hydronium" title="Hydronium">hydronium</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interaction_of_free_protons_with_ordinary_matter">Interaction of free protons with ordinary matter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Interaction of free protons with ordinary matter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although protons have affinity for oppositely charged electrons, this is a relatively low-energy interaction and so free protons must lose sufficient velocity (and <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>) in order to become closely associated and bound to electrons. High energy protons, in traversing ordinary matter, lose energy by collisions with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_nuclei" title="Atomic nuclei">atomic nuclei</a>, and by <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">ionization</a> of atoms (removing electrons) until they are slowed sufficiently to be captured by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_cloud" title="Electron cloud">electron cloud</a> in a normal atom.
</p><p>However, in such an association with an electron, the character of the bound proton is not changed, and it remains a proton. The attraction of low-energy free protons to any electrons present in normal matter (such as the electrons in normal atoms) causes free protons to stop and to form a new chemical bond with an atom. Such a bond happens at any sufficiently "cold" temperature (that is, comparable to temperatures at the surface of the Sun) and with any type of atom. Thus, in interaction with any type of normal (non-plasma) matter, low-velocity free protons do not remain free but are attracted to electrons in any atom or molecule with which they come into contact, causing the proton and molecule to combine. Such molecules are then said to be "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protonated" title="Protonated">protonated</a>", and chemically they are simply compounds of hydrogen, often positively charged. Often, as a result, they become so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Br%C3%B8nsted_acid" title="Brønsted acid">Brønsted acids</a>. For example, a proton captured by a water molecule in water becomes <a href="/wiki/Hydronium" title="Hydronium">hydronium</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aqueous" title="Aqueous">aqueous</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cation" title="Cation">cation</a> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub>O<sup class="template-chem2-sup">+</sup></span>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Proton_in_chemistry">Proton in chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Proton in chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atomic_number">Atomic number</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Atomic number">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, the number of protons in the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> of an atom is known as the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>, which determines the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> to which the atom belongs. For example, the atomic number of <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> is 17; this means that each chlorine atom has 17 protons and that all atoms with 17 protons are chlorine atoms. The chemical properties of each atom are determined by the number of (negatively charged) <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>, which for neutral atoms is equal to the number of (positive) protons so that the total charge is zero. For example, a neutral chlorine atom has 17 protons and 17 electrons, whereas a Cl<sup>−</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anion" title="Anion">anion</a> has 17 protons and 18 electrons for a total charge of −1.
</p><p>All atoms of a given element are not necessarily identical, however. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Number_of_neutrons" title="Number of neutrons">number of neutrons</a> may vary to form different <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>, and energy levels may differ, resulting in different <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomers</a>. For example, there are two stable <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_chlorine" title="Isotopes of chlorine">isotopes of chlorine</a>: <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">35</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">17</sub></span>Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> with 35 − 17 = 18 neutrons and <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">37</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">17</sub></span>Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> with 37 − 17 = 20 neutrons.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen_ion">Hydrogen ion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Hydrogen ion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hydron_(chemistry)" title="Hydron (chemistry)">Hydron (chemistry)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="230" data-file-width="230" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Hydrogen.svg/220px-Hydrogen.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Hydrogen.svg/330px-Hydrogen.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Hydrogen.svg/440px-Hydrogen.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Protium, the most common isotope of hydrogen, consists of one proton and one electron (it has no neutrons). The term "hydrogen ion" (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>) implies that that H-atom has lost its one electron, causing only a proton to remain. Thus, in chemistry, the terms "proton" and "hydrogen ion" (for the protium isotope) are used synonymously</div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:20%; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>The proton is a unique chemical species, being a bare nucleus. As a consequence it has no independent existence in the condensed state and is invariably found bound by a pair of electrons to another atom.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">Ross Stewart, <i>The Proton: Application to Organic Chemistry</i> (1985, p. 1)</cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>In chemistry, the term proton refers to the hydrogen ion, <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>. Since the atomic number of hydrogen is 1, a hydrogen ion has no electrons and corresponds to a bare nucleus, consisting of a proton (and 0 neutrons for the most abundant isotope <i>protium</i> <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen-1" title="Hydrogen-1"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>). The proton is a "bare charge" with only about 1/64,000 of the radius of a hydrogen atom, and so is extremely reactive chemically. The free proton, thus, has an extremely short lifetime in chemical systems such as liquids and it reacts immediately with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_cloud" title="Electron cloud">electron cloud</a> of any available molecule. In aqueous solution, it forms the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydronium_ion" title="Hydronium ion">hydronium ion</a>, H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup>, which in turn is further <a href="/wiki/Solvation" title="Solvation">solvated</a> by water molecules in <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_ion#Cation_(positively_charged)" title="Hydrogen ion">clusters</a> such as [H<sub>5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>]<sup>+</sup> and [H<sub>9</sub>O<sub>4</sub>]<sup>+</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Headrick2005_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Headrick2005-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>The transfer of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> in an <a href="/wiki/Br%C3%B8nsted%E2%80%93Lowry_acid%E2%80%93base_theory" title="Brønsted–Lowry acid–base theory">acid–base reaction</a> is usually referred to as "proton transfer". The <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acid</a> is referred to as a proton donor and the <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">base</a> as a proton acceptor. Likewise, <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemical</a> terms such as <a href="/wiki/Proton_pump" title="Proton pump">proton pump</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_channel" title="Proton channel">proton channel</a> refer to the movement of hydrated <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> ions.
</p><p>The ion produced by removing the electron from a <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> atom is known as a deuteron, not a proton. Likewise, removing an electron from a <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> atom produces a triton.
</p>
<h3><span id="Proton_nuclear_magnetic_resonance_.28NMR.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Proton_nuclear_magnetic_resonance_(NMR)">Proton nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Proton nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Also in chemistry, the term "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_NMR" title="Proton NMR">proton NMR</a>" refers to the observation of hydrogen-1 nuclei in (mostly <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic</a>) molecules by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance">nuclear magnetic resonance</a>. This method uses the <a href="/wiki/Quantization_(physics)" title="Quantization (physics)">quantized</a> <a href="/wiki/Spin_magnetic_moment" title="Spin magnetic moment">spin magnetic moment</a> of the proton, which is due to its angular momentum (or <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a>), which in turn has a magnitude of one-half the reduced <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">Planck constant</a>. (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \hbar /2}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi class="MJX-variant">ℏ<!-- ℏ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \hbar /2}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\hbar /2" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/74e0713309590b82aed3a8247c30cfdeae9f456b" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:3.631ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>). The name refers to examination of protons as they occur in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen-1" title="Hydrogen-1">protium</a> (hydrogen-1 atoms) in compounds, and does not imply that free protons exist in the compound being studied.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_exposure">Human exposure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Human exposure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_body" title="Effect of spaceflight on the body">Effect of spaceflight on the body</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Proton_therapy" title="Proton therapy">Proton therapy</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Surface_Experiments_Package" title="Apollo Lunar Surface Experiments Package">Apollo Lunar Surface Experiments Packages</a> (ALSEP) determined that more than 95% of the particles in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> are electrons and protons, in approximately equal numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LPI2009_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-LPI2009-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BNL2007_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-BNL2007-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Because the Solar Wind <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">Spectrometer</a> made continuous measurements, it was possible to measure how the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a> affects arriving solar wind particles. For about two-thirds of each orbit, the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> is outside of the Earth's magnetic field. At these times, a typical proton density was 10 to 20 per cubic centimeter, with most protons having velocities between 400 and 650 kilometers per second. For about five days of each month, the Moon is inside the Earth's geomagnetic tail, and typically no solar wind particles were detectable. For the remainder of each lunar orbit, the Moon is in a transitional region known as the <a href="/wiki/Magnetosheath" title="Magnetosheath">magnetosheath</a>, where the Earth's magnetic field affects the solar wind, but does not completely exclude it. In this region, the particle flux is reduced, with typical proton velocities of 250 to 450 kilometers per second. During the lunar night, the spectrometer was shielded from the solar wind by the Moon and no solar wind particles were measured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LPI2009_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-LPI2009-52">[52]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Protons also have extrasolar origin from galactic <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>, where they make up about 90% of the total particle flux. These protons often have higher energy than solar wind protons, and their intensity is far more uniform and less variable than protons coming from the Sun, the production of which is heavily affected by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_proton_event" title="Solar proton event">solar proton events</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">coronal mass ejections</a>.
</p><p>Research has been performed on the dose-rate effects of protons, as typically found in <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">space travel</a>, on human health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BNL2007_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-BNL2007-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shukitt-Hale_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shukitt-Hale-54">[54]</a></sup> To be more specific, there are hopes to identify what specific chromosomes are damaged, and to define the damage, during <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> development from proton exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BNL2007_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-BNL2007-53">[53]</a></sup> Another study looks into determining "the effects of exposure to proton irradiation on neurochemical and behavioral endpoints, including <a href="/wiki/Dopaminergic" title="Dopaminergic">dopaminergic</a> functioning, <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">amphetamine</a>-induced conditioned taste aversion learning, and spatial learning and memory as measured by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morris_water_maze" title="Morris water maze">Morris water maze</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shukitt-Hale_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shukitt-Hale-54">[54]</a></sup> Electrical charging of a spacecraft due to interplanetary proton bombardment has also been proposed for study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green2006_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Green2006-55">[55]</a></sup> There are many more studies that pertain to space travel, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_cosmic_rays" title="Galactic cosmic rays">galactic cosmic rays</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Health_threat_from_cosmic_rays" title="Health threat from cosmic rays">possible health effects</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_proton_event" title="Solar proton event">solar proton event</a> exposure.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/STS-65#Mission_parameters" title="STS-65">American Biostack and Soviet Biorack</a> space travel experiments have demonstrated the severity of molecular damage induced by heavy ions on <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artemia" title="Artemia">Artemia</a> cysts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planel2004_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planel2004-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiproton">Antiproton</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Antiproton">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Antiproton" title="Antiproton">Antiproton</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CPT-symmetry" title="CPT-symmetry">CPT-symmetry</a> puts strong constraints on the relative properties of particles and <a href="/wiki/Antiparticle" title="Antiparticle">antiparticles</a> and, therefore, is open to stringent tests. For example, the charges of a proton and antiproton must sum to exactly zero. This equality has been tested to one part in <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7008100000000000000♠"></span>10<sup>8</sup></span>. The equality of their masses has also been tested to better than one part in <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7008100000000000000♠"></span>10<sup>8</sup></span>. By holding antiprotons in a <a href="/wiki/Penning_trap" title="Penning trap">Penning trap</a>, the equality of the charge-to-mass ratio of protons and antiprotons has been tested to one part in <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7009600000000000000♠"></span>6<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>9</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gabrielse2006_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gabrielse2006-57">[57]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_moment" title="Magnetic moment">magnetic moment</a> of antiprotons has been measured with error of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6997800000000000000♠"></span>8<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−3</sup></span> nuclear <a href="/wiki/Bohr_magneton" title="Bohr magneton">Bohr magnetons</a>, and is found to be equal and opposite to that of a proton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Proton&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
